<div id="review_155174342" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155174342" />
  <a href="/user/show/2846441-karla" class="left imgcol" title="Karla"><img alt="Karla" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1413656093p2/2846441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/155174342?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2846441-karla" class="user" title="Karla">Karla</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/379262.The_Name_of_the_Rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780446344104">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=cover-artists-sanjulian" class="actionLinkLite">cover-artists-sanjulian</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=medieval" class="actionLinkLite">medieval</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=historical-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">historical-mystery</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=1st-person-pov" class="actionLinkLite">1st-person-pov</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=banging-aint-just-for-the-pulpit" class="actionLinkLite">banging-aint-just-for-the-pulpit</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=book-to-film" class="actionLinkLite">book-to-film</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155174342" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14245985323060447473">A lot has already been said about this for me to rehash the basics or try to address the themes (and my pathetic attempts to sound SMRT would be embarrassing), so I'll simply recount the feels I had.<br><br>* All that theological debating? More fascinating than I'd ever thought possible.<br><br>* Medieval church history! OMG, you medieval guys, God's representative on Earth was a corrupt motherfucker. But I guess the Holy Hot Pincers of Chastisement kept you all nodding your heads and staying in line (more or l</span>
  <span id="freeText14245985323060447473" style="display:none">A lot has already been said about this for me to rehash the basics or try to address the themes (and my pathetic attempts to sound SMRT would be embarrassing), so I'll simply recount the feels I had.<br><br>* All that theological debating? More fascinating than I'd ever thought possible.<br><br>* Medieval church history! OMG, you medieval guys, God's representative on Earth was a corrupt motherfucker. But I guess the Holy Hot Pincers of Chastisement kept you all nodding your heads and staying in line (more or less).<br><br>* The huge amount of splinter sects and "heretical" offshoots are mind-boggling, and they're all convinced the others are wrong because The Popular Front thinks Christ only farted on Sundays and the People's Front insists it was Thursday afternoons after the tea &amp; cucumber sammiches or some such crap. No wonder the Middle Ages were a mess (and continuing a long tradition of dogmatic asshattery).<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380347143/701509.jpg" width="400" height="225" class="escapedImg"><br><em>Follow the gourd! Following the sandal is just silly.</em><br><br>* While all the semiotic stuff whizzed right over my head, I was still glued to the pages and didn't skip a word and it didn't feel like a chore. *pats self on back*<br><br>* Cool! The last part of the book was slightly different than the movie! Even so, the fact that the mystery plot itself adhered closely to the book didn't make it boring or a feeling of arrgh-I've-seen-this-already. On the contrary, there was so much new stuff with the history and theology that the whole thing was super-fresh and exciting.<br><br>* The humor. I loved Adso's narration and the coming-of-age aspect of it, as well as his older-self-looking-back-on-his-younger-self perspective. He and William had a great rapport (another thing that the movie nailed).<br><br>* That abbey was one gay place.<br><br>As is obvious, I'd seen the movie before reading the book, and I think it made the reading experience a bit easier so I could focus on the theological debates, the history, and Eco's narrative style. I'd recommend seeing the movie first, especially if you're approaching the book as a random read and not as a literary scholar (which I ain't, as is obvious).<br><br>I greatly enjoyed it and am glad that I finally sacked up and read it after being intimidated by it for so long.<br><br><strong>ETA:</strong> Whoops...Rosemary Rogers. I mentioned that in my last update. OK, so, this book....awesome and page-turning! I understand what's going on and being said as I read, but just TRY to tell someone else what's going on and what's being said? Pffft. Forget it. Read it for yourself. That's been the exact same experience I've had with every Rosemary Rogers book I've ever read.<br><br>If these two ever collaborated on a book together, my head would esplode.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14245985323060447473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>

<div id="review_26941407" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26941407" />
  <a href="/user/show/1262683-terry" class="left imgcol" title="Terry "><img alt="Terry " src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26941407?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1262683-terry" class="user" title="Terry ">Terry </a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/335061.The_Name_of_the_Rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330284141">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26941407" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17181476162605425081">This is one of my 're-read often' books. The story of Adso and his mentor Brother William as they encounter the nefarious secrets of the abbey they have journeyed to somewhere in the heart of Italy is fantastic. Brother William is the unflappable, Sherlock-like investigator first asked by the Abbot to look into some mysterious deaths and then told to stop when he gets too near the truth. Driven by his hunger for knowledge, William attempts to see beyond the rumours of apocalypse and the presence</span>
  <span id="freeText17181476162605425081" style="display:none">This is one of my 're-read often' books. The story of Adso and his mentor Brother William as they encounter the nefarious secrets of the abbey they have journeyed to somewhere in the heart of Italy is fantastic. Brother William is the unflappable, Sherlock-like investigator first asked by the Abbot to look into some mysterious deaths and then told to stop when he gets too near the truth. Driven by his hunger for knowledge, William attempts to see beyond the rumours of apocalypse and the presence of the devil to find the true human evil at the heart of the mystery.<br><br>Following William is his innocent companion, the novice Adso, who tells us this tale of his youth from the perspective of old age. In this tour-de-force by Eco we come across a motley crew of strange characters from the pitable and beastly Salvatore, and the urbane and pompous Abbot Abo to the driven zealot Bernardo Gui (an actualy historical figure painted in somewhat extreme colours by Eco). Woven into the mystery plot and panoply of characters is a dazzling mix of medieval politics and philosophy as the abbey is playing host to a meeting between rival theological factions: the Franciscans who hope to validate their beliefes and way of life in the midst of controversy and their enemies who hope to squash this rival group in its infancy.<br><br>To me, reading Eco has been an experience of diminishing returns, as each novel he has written seems to have fallen further and further from the heights achieved by this novel until I could not even finish _Baudolino_ due to its unreadability. That being said _The Name of the Rose_ is a classic and I highly encourage any and all to enter into its fascinating world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17181476162605425081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100256636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100256636" />
  <a href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson" class="left imgcol" title="Kevin Neilson"><img alt="Kevin Neilson" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1242063463p2/2088163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/100256636?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 27, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson" class="user" title="Kevin Neilson">Kevin Neilson</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>



        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100256636" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5825554703684847874">What a didactic, tedious, prolix piece of trash!  Eco writes whole paragraphs in Latin and then leaves them untranslated, because he's such an awesome polyglot that chicks want to do him.  Readers are also expected to know Dutch.  Eco likes to hear himself talk, too.  Want to hear pedantic 14th-century theological arguments that stretch on for pages and have nothing to do with the plot?  You've got it!  Want a lame Dan Brown mystery, with the same stilted dialogue, but embellished with entire chapters</span>
  <span id="freeText5825554703684847874" style="display:none">What a didactic, tedious, prolix piece of trash!  Eco writes whole paragraphs in Latin and then leaves them untranslated, because he's such an awesome polyglot that chicks want to do him.  Readers are also expected to know Dutch.  Eco likes to hear himself talk, too.  Want to hear pedantic 14th-century theological arguments that stretch on for pages and have nothing to do with the plot?  You've got it!  Want a lame Dan Brown mystery, with the same stilted dialogue, but embellished with entire chapters of the author showing off how much trivia he knows about ancient Arab codices?  No problem!  The guy is such a tool that this is how he describes himself on the jacket:  <em>Umberto Eco is a world-famous specialist in semiotics, a distinguished historian, philosopher, aesthetician, and scholar whose interests range from St. Thomas Aquinas to James Joyce to Superman.</em>  I'm going to hurl.  Aesthetician?  Please.  More like <em>ass</em>thetician.<br><br>Do you think I'm exaggerating?  I've proof:  I've copied this terrible passage wherein the hero discovers how to enter the secret chamber, using his knowledge of the genitive case in Latin.  Be careful not to hurl as you read this. <br><br><blockquote>[<em>The old Monk, William, and the narrator apprentice, Adso, are hiding out in the stables.</em>:]<br>To his right, the the third animal in line raised his head, sensing our presence, and whinnied.  I smiled.  "Tertius equi," I said.<br>    "What?" William asked.<br>    "Nothing.  I was remembering poor Salvatore.  He wanted to perform God knows what magic with that horse, and, with his fractured Latin he called him 'tertius equi.'  Which would be the u."<br>    "The u?" asked William, who had heard my prattle without paying much attention to it.<br>    "Yes, because 'tertiu equi' does not mean the third horse, but the third of the horse, and the third letter of the word 'equus' is u.  But this is all nonsense..."<br>    William looked at me, and in the darkness I seemed to see his face transformed.  "God bless you, Adso!" he said to me.  "Why, of course, suppositio materialis, the discourse is presumed de dicto and not de re....What a fool I am!"  He gave himself such a great blow on the forehead that I heard a clap, and I believe he hurt himself.  "My boy, this is the second time today that wisdom has spoken through your mouth, first in dream and now in waking!  Run, run to your cell and fetch the lamp, or, rather, both lamps we hid.  Let no one see you, and join me in the church at once!  Ask no questions."<br>...<br>    I ran into the church.  William was under the tripod and was rereading the parchment with Venantius's notes.<br>    "Adso," he said to me, "'primum et septimum de equator' does not mean the first and seventh of four, but of the four, the word 'four'!"  For a moment I still did not understand, but then I was enlightened:  "Super thronos viginti quatuor!  The writing!  The verse!  The words are carved over the mirror!"<br>    "Come," William said, "perhaps we are still in time to save a life!"<br>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="5825554703684847874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>

<div id="review_62185754" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62185754" />
  <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="left imgcol" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420066396p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/62185754?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="user" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/611484.The_Name_of_the_Rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099466031">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer62185754'); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62185754" class="readable" style="display:none">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13663346646825880214">I spent 1 full week reading this book. Not only because it has almost 500 dense pages but also because I suffered a terrible backache while reading this. So I had to turn and change my sitting or lying position every few minutes while reading.<br><br>The story revolves around William of Baskervilles and inquisitor and Adso a novice monk. They were ask to investigate the killing that happened in a rich Italian abbey during the medieval period (1327). It is a mix of whodunnit (like the recent novel I read</span>
  <span id="freeText13663346646825880214" style="display:none">I spent 1 full week reading this book. Not only because it has almost 500 dense pages but also because I suffered a terrible backache while reading this. So I had to turn and change my sitting or lying position every few minutes while reading.<br><br>The story revolves around William of Baskervilles and inquisitor and Adso a novice monk. They were ask to investigate the killing that happened in a rich Italian abbey during the medieval period (1327). It is a mix of whodunnit (like the recent novel I read of Agatha Christie, THE MURDER OF ROGER ACKROYD) and historical novel set in medieval period (like Ken Follett's PILLARS OF THE EARTH). Naturally, I started leafing the pages with gusto because I liked both novels.<br><br>However, there are just too many details and Latin phrases in the book that can annoy readers who are not fully familiar with that period of European history and that dying language. Also, after the pattern seemingly being followed by the "killer" was later revealed to be a series of coincidences only, the magic of being a mystery novel just vanished. It is as if the climax was built for nothing. With me spending 400+ pages of understanding the details of the pattern was a bit disappointing.<br><br>The narrative is beautiful though. Considering that this was just translated from the original language it was written, Italian is an achievement for the translator. The reason is that the English prose retained its cadence and appropriateness to its ancient Italian setting. It has that feeling of being a classic despite the novel having been written by Umberto Eco only in the early 80's.<br><br>Lately, I have been reading novels written several decades ago or with settings way back many centuries ago and sometimes this makes me feel ancient too. I should read something very contemporary next to this. Otherwise, my backpain might decide to stay as my brain thinks that I am already a senior citizen and having body aches is just but natural. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13663346646825880214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_143356950" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143356950" />
  <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="left imgcol" title="Aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1419544181p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/143356950?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 19, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="user" title="Aubrey">Aubrey</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/379262.The_Name_of_the_Rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780446344104">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1001-06-12" class="actionLinkLite">1001-06-12</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star" class="actionLinkLite">4-star</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated" class="actionLinkLite">translated</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1000-guardian" class="actionLinkLite">1000-guardian</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=italian" class="actionLinkLite">italian</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1001-self" class="actionLinkLite">1001-self</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated-dp" class="actionLinkLite">translated-dp</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2012" class="actionLinkLite">r-2012</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads" class="actionLinkLite">r-goodreads</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143356950" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18257047322134613869">I have never been so undecided as to what to rate a book. The oscillation from three to four stars and back again was dizzying, and made worse by the fact that I felt that a 3.5 would be a cop out concerning this particular novel. <br><br>The writing was a mix of excellent logical processes and long trains of theological meanderings. I'd find the words blurring before my eyes when the author kept up his lists for too long, or when one of the characters was especially verbose on religious concerns. At le</span>
  <span id="freeText18257047322134613869" style="display:none">I have never been so undecided as to what to rate a book. The oscillation from three to four stars and back again was dizzying, and made worse by the fact that I felt that a 3.5 would be a cop out concerning this particular novel. <br><br>The writing was a mix of excellent logical processes and long trains of theological meanderings. I'd find the words blurring before my eyes when the author kept up his lists for too long, or when one of the characters was especially verbose on religious concerns. At least I have some familiarity with the Bible and its related texts, or I would have been completely lost.<br><br> It was odd, though. It almost seemed that the author planned it that way, as detective monk William of Baskerville would often give insights into how ridiculous the whole concept of religion is. This ridiculousness was very apparent in the debates over laughter, heresy, and the differences between the countless denominations. So perhaps it was intended. Made for some very boring passages, I will admit. But the brilliant flashes of common sense and amazing trains of thought were enough to tide me over. I only wish more of the book was like that.<br><br>But I must say, blatant inspiration is blatant. It's been a while since I've read the Sherlock Holmes books, so I'll be modeling my observations after my more recent watching of the BBC series (unorthodox, I know, but it illustrates my point). The overwhelming powers of observation and subsequent deduction, the use of drugs to ameliorate thinking, the meditative states that heightened these neurological processes to a sharp focal point, and especially the final relationship between William and the revealed villain. That heady mix of moral antagonism and intellectual respect was very, very familiar. <br><br>It's a shame that it didn't last, the intelligent state of mind at any rate. William's novice had flashes of insight only when in the presence of his mentor, and the manuscript he writes shows how far back he has slipped into the comfort of religious ignorance. He saw the sense of writing down what happened, at any rate. And his discomfort with his state of mind in view of his memories shows that he isn't completely resigned to the sheep state of blind faith. <br><br>But that doesn't really matter. What matters is that I have a couple more books by this author to get through, and the reading should be interesting if they are as filled with convoluted and contrasting passages as this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18257047322134613869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_180913221" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180913221" />
  <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="left imgcol" title="Sue"><img alt="Sue" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/180913221?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 13, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="user" title="Sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/8694005-the-name-of-the-rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition ">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=italy" class="actionLinkLite">italy</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=medieval" class="actionLinkLite">medieval</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180913221" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5659167373885304990">The question becomes-how to review a book that has been read and reviewed by so many, a book that is so well known. Short and sweet I think!<br><br>The basic story is both simple and complex (well there goes my above-stated aim). Brother William of Baskerville has been sent to an Italian abbey of some renown on what is an important and urgent mission, to investigate the possibility of heresy present in that Franciscan setting. William formerly served in the "forces" of the Inquisition but chose to leave</span>
  <span id="freeText5659167373885304990" style="display:none">The question becomes-how to review a book that has been read and reviewed by so many, a book that is so well known. Short and sweet I think!<br><br>The basic story is both simple and complex (well there goes my above-stated aim). Brother William of Baskerville has been sent to an Italian abbey of some renown on what is an important and urgent mission, to investigate the possibility of heresy present in that Franciscan setting. William formerly served in the "forces" of the Inquisition but chose to leave that "august" body lacking their rabid style and beliefs. On arriving at the Abbey, he finds that he has an added problem to solve, mysterious death.<br><br>William's character is the delight of this Medieval novel. He thinks; he questions; he reasons; he believes and has faith; but he also has doubts and uses science and his brain. He is a pre-Renaissance man. With William on his trek is a novice, Adso, the narrator of the story, putting pen to paper later in life. He is William's sounding board and sometime-accomplice.<br><br>So much happens that is of the mind here, and Eco is indeed brilliant at (re)creating ecclesiastical arguments for and against laughter, poverty (and degrees of poverty) and Christ's views on poverty(!). At first I was intimidated but gradually I realized what these arguments were about. Power...the oldest source of argument in the world. I was interested to see the familiar name of Aristotle raised after reading the unfamiliar names of the many heretics. And then there are the murders...what purpose could there be for them. Says William:<br><br><br><em>"In that face, deformed by hatred of philosophy, I saw<br>     for the first time the portrait of the Antichrist....The<br>     Antichrist can be born from piety itself, from excessive<br>     love of God or of the truth, as the heretic is born from<br>     the saint and the possessed from the seer. Fear prophets,<br>     Adso, and those prepared to die for the truth, for as a <br>     rule they make many others die with them, often before <br>     them, at times instead of them."</em>  (loc 7983)<br><br><br>Brother William and Umberto Eco could be speaking today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5659167373885304990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100843575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100843575" />
  <a href="/user/show/2569242-kathrina" class="left imgcol" title="Kathrina"><img alt="Kathrina" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1336653486p2/2569242.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/100843575?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 13, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2569242-kathrina" class="user" title="Kathrina">Kathrina</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/10489.The_Name_of_the_Rose" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446322188">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=great-literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">great-literary-fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=horror-thriller" class="actionLinkLite">horror-thriller</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=italian" class="actionLinkLite">italian</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2569242-kathrina?shelf=nyrb-intro-authors" class="actionLinkLite">nyrb-intro-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100843575" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5836205704196069358">This book is so self-aware, I feel like it's reading over my shoulder right now. I recall, about half-way through, lamenting the fact that my edition contained no footnotes for translating the frequent Latin, Italian, and Greek texts thrown into this narrative. And now, after reaching Eco's last line (in Latin!) I hear him laughing at me. Don't you get it, reader?! Roger Bacon chides the scholar who doesn't make languages his first priority, and as I can only piece together a few Latin words and</span>
  <span id="freeText5836205704196069358" style="display:none">This book is so self-aware, I feel like it's reading over my shoulder right now. I recall, about half-way through, lamenting the fact that my edition contained no footnotes for translating the frequent Latin, Italian, and Greek texts thrown into this narrative. And now, after reaching Eco's last line (in Latin!) I hear him laughing at me. Don't you get it, reader?! Roger Bacon chides the scholar who doesn't make languages his first priority, and as I can only piece together a few Latin words and stretch meaning from the context, I come to my own conclusions based on what I have read and learned in the past, as William solves his case the wrong way, but with the right answers. I don't read Latin as Adso doesn't read Greek, as William doesn't read Arabic, as mortal man can never read God's language, and so we make our own assumptions based on what we do know. The world's knowledge is constantly fragmented, and we keep what we can use, we forget or reinvent. One day the library will burn, the database will fail, and the only books that will matter are the ones we've read. And who can read what books? Here I begin my <br><br>            Ode to the Joys of Reading Two Books Simultaneously<br><br>I picked up this book <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5189.Reading_With_Oprah__The_Book_Club_That_Changed_America" title="Reading With Oprah: The Book Club That Changed America by Kathleen Rooney">Reading With Oprah: The Book Club That Changed America</a>, not because I am necessarily interested in the particular books that comprise Oprah's list, but because the phenomenon, as a bookseller, intrigues me. My own attitude as book snob was outed when Cormac McCarthy joined her ranks, and I felt the mass public didn't deserve his offering. You have to earn an appreciation for McCarthy. What the hell do I mean by that? Here, watch me struggle with my conscience: If you tell me your favorite author is Picoult, my recommendation will never be McCarthy, even though I feel he'll always be a more satisfying read. But you're not ready. You don't want to reorganize the furniture in your head, you just want a touch of color on your sofa. And that's ok. Moving furniture is a lot of hard work, and I like an easy read between moves. But my favorite reason to read is to change; that's not everyone's reason. What does this have to do with Name of the Rose, you ask?! Because there was this monk, 600 years ago, running Oprah's book club, and he didn't want <s>McCarthy</s>, I mean, Aristotle, distributed to the masses, because they weren't going to read it right, even if it is in Greek. The book had the ability to cause change. This monk, this librarian, didn't want to cause change, to innovate, he wanted to preserve, to archive. Oprah says she wants change, but by promoting her book list, she's preserving at the same time, labeling titles to the Oprah cult, garnering huge sales for her authors. And now she's quit. What may have been future Oprah picks are hidden in the finis Africae, left only for the chosen to find. My point? I dunno. But Eco tells us that all books are references to other books, and now <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93269.Earth_Abides" title="Earth Abides by George R. Stewart">Earth Abides</a>speaks to me about the end of reading, preserving, and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6419887.The_Boy_Who_Harnessed_the_Wind__Creating_Currents_of_Electricity_and_Hope" title="The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind: Creating Currents of Electricity and Hope by William Kamkwamba">The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind: Creating Currents of Electricity and Hope</a> speaks to me about the salvation of reading, innovation, and every damn thing I read reminds me why I read and why the book I haven't read yet is going to make the book I just read even better. I'm filling in the context with my own collection of fragments, and the story is told and told again, and when I'm ready I'm going to move the sofa.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5836205704196069358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

  document.onclick = checkOpenSelects;

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uN5ooeIHFk8h+o02d1J0Rb5jF9gK9NhZcEgrCE6+Q+cLG9pSglscmYgqUIebyqSdiNHkkz8ffHYV8YiV1hEiTQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H+G02H4KrWIOBu0n4wFEjcfjHkrdV/7BLA/8d3oy282sJAYrHlantKfWMJYPmZRV8VHtAei8Wu5Jtl/q4p26Zw==')})
  }
</script>

The dramatic sweep and richness of observation, the seamless intertwining of public and private worlds, and the grasp of human frailty imbue The Leopard with its particular melancholy beauty and power, and place it among the greatest historical novels of our time. Although Giuseppe di Lampedusa had long had the book in mind, he began writing it only in his late fifties; he died at age sixty, soon after the manuscript was rejected as unpublishable. In his introduction, Gioacchino Lanza Tomasi, Lampedusa\'s nephew, gives us a detailed history of the initial publication and the various editions that followed. And he includes passages Lampedusa wrote for the book that were omitted by the original Italian editors. Historical novel set in 1st-century-AD Rome by Robert Graves, published in 1934. The book is written as an autobiographical memoir by Roman emperor Claudius. Physically weak, afflicted with stammering, and inclined to drool, Claudius is an embarrassment to his family and is shunted to the background of imperial affairs. The benefits of his seeming ineffectuality are twofold: he becomes a scholar and historian, and he is spared the worst cruelties inflicted on the imperial family by its own members during the reigns of Augustus, Tiberius, and Caligula. Palace intrigues and murders surround him. Claudius\' informal narration serves to emphasize the banality of the imperial family\'s endless greed and lust. The story concludes with Claudius ascending to the imperial throne. A sequel, Claudius, the God and His Wife Messalina (1935), covers Claudius\' years as Roman emperor.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6753256734385457468\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));;     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1730.Umberto_Eco">About Umberto Eco</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1730.Umberto_Eco"><img alt="1730" class="right" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1422009610p2/1730.jpg" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer18150954378595273726">Umberto Eco is an Italian writer of fiction, essays, academic texts, and children's books, and certainly one of the finest authors of the twentieth century. A professor of semiotics at the University of Bologna, Ecoâs brilliant fiction is known for its playful use of language and symbols, its astonishing array of allusions and references, and clever use of puzzles and narrative inventions. His per</span>
  <span id="freeText18150954378595273726" style="display:none">Umberto Eco is an Italian writer of fiction, essays, academic texts, and children's books, and certainly one of the finest authors of the twentieth century. A professor of semiotics at the University of Bologna, Ecoâs brilliant fiction is known for its playful use of language and symbols, its astonishing array of allusions and references, and clever use of puzzles and narrative inventions. His perceptive essays on modern culture are filled with a delightful sense of humor and irony, and his ideas on semiotics, interpretation, and aesthetics have established his reputation as one of academiaâs foremost thinkers.</span>
